Classification:
- Manipulated

Identified Attack:  
- Most Likely: Attack 7  
- Confidence: High  
- Reasoning: The query day exhibits a preservation of the normal diurnal pattern but with all values uniformly reduced and multiple zero readings during the originally low-consumption early-morning period. This exactly matches the mechanism of Attack 7 – a constant offset subtracted from every sample, with negatives clamped to zero. Attack 1 (constant scaling) and Attack 3 (per-sample random scaling) cannot produce repeated exact zeros or a flat block of zero. Attack 2 (on-off zeroing) does not explain the diminished non-zero values; Attack 4 would leave some period at normal consumption; Attack 6 (capping) would not generate zeros; Attack IEEE would retain original amplitudes in un-zeroed intervals.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- The attacker subtracts a fixed cut-off value (~5.64) from the actual consumption readings of the day. Any sample that becomes negative is reported as 0. This preserves the overall trend (daily rise and fall) but lowers the entire profile and introduces zeros during the morning trough.
- Magnitude is reduced significantly (mean drops from ~7.54 to 2.76, max from 13.57 to 7.93), while the shape of the diurnal curve is retained. Zeros appear in a block where original values were below the cut-off.
- Possible technical causes: false data injection, reprogramming of the meter to report consumption relative to an offset, or a sophisticated meter bypass that implements a fixed subtraction before reporting.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are asked to analyze a query day (DAY_ID=3) against a reference week of normal consumption. The reference week has 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6). The query day is DAY_ID=3 but note that in the reference week Day 3 corresponds to a Wednesday (dow=2) and dom=3. The query day also has DAY_ID=3 but it's from a different month (cal_month=6 vs 5) and dom=17, so it's a different day. We need to determine if the consumption is manipulated or original.

First, let's examine the per-day statistics:
Reference week days have means around 7.54 for Day 3, ranging from 7.28 to 8.87. Mins are around 3.52-5.53, maxes around 12.56-14.57.
Query day: min=0.00, max=7.93, std=1.70, mean=2.76. Mean is much lower than any reference day. Min is 0.00, which is unusual (reference days have min >= 3.52). Max is 7.93, which is lower than typical maxes around 12-15. So the consumption is significantly reduced, and there are zero values.

Now let's look at the temporal features: Query day is a Wednesday (dow=2) like Day 3 in the reference week, but different date. So we might compare with Day 3's pattern, but also with the overall pattern of the week. The query day shows many low values and zeros towards the beginning (readings from midnight to early morning). Specifically, the query day readings start with 3.41, then drop to 1.90, etc., and zeros appear at index 23, 24, 25 (based on 15-min intervals, 96 readings, zeros at positions 0:00? Let's map indices to time: 96 readings, so 0-3 is first hour, etc. indices 0-3: 3.41,3.41,3.41,3.91; then 4-7: 2.41,1.90,2.41,1.90; etc. Zeros at indices 23,24,25,26? Actually sequence: ...1.40, 1.40, 0.00, 0.00, 0.00, 0.40, 0.40... So zeros at indices 23-25? Let's count: reading numbers:
0:3.41,1:3.41,2:3.41,3:3.91

12:1.90,13:1.40,14:0.90,15:1.90
16:0.90,17:0.90,18:1.40,19:0.90
20:1.90,21:1.40,22:1.40,23:0.00

28:1.40,29:0.40,30:0.90,31:2.41
... So zeros in the early morning between roughly 5:45 and 6:15? Actually 23rd reading is at 5:45 (since 24*15min = 6 hours, reading 0 is 0:00-0:15, reading 23 is 5:45-6:00). Then zeros at 24 (6:00-6:15), 25 (6:15-6:30? No, careful: reading 0: 0:00-0:15, 1:0:15-0:30, 2:0:30-0:45, 3:0:45-1:00. So reading 23: 5:45-6:00, 24:6:00-6:15, 25:6:15-6:30, 26:6:30-6:45, 27:6:45-7:00. So zeros from 5:45 to 6:30, then 0.40. So a block of zeros. Later, no zeros but low values until a peak in the late morning? Actually there's a peak around reading indices 76-77: 7.43, 7.93, etc. That's around 7:00 PM? 96/24 = 4 per hour, so reading 76 is 19:00-19:15 (since 0:00 start, reading 0). So the highest consumption is 7.93 at 19:15-19:30 maybe. Compared to reference days, peaks are usually around 12-14. So the whole day is scaled down and includes a zero block.

Which attack types could produce this? 
- Attack 0: all zero. Not the case, only some zeros, not all.
- Attack 1: multiply by constant between 0 and 1. This would reduce all values proportionally, but would not introduce zeros unless original values were zero. Original min is 4.02 etc., no zeros. So if multiplied by constant, all values would be >0. Attack 1 cannot produce zeros. Not consistent.
- Attack 2: on-off attack, zeros for random duration. Yes, there is a zero block of duration ~1 hour. That matches Attack 2: "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration". This could cause zeros and also may leave other parts unchanged? But here other values are lower than normal, but not zeroed. However Attack 2 description: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." It doesn't say anything about scaling other values. It just replaces some with zero. But in the query day, non-zero values are also significantly lower than normal (mean 2.76 vs 7.54). If only a random duration is zeroed, the overall mean would be reduced but still the non-zero segments should look like normal consumption proportions. Let's check: In reference Day 3, the early morning (pre-6am) values are around 6-7? Actually Day 3's early morning readings: 
Day 3: 7.54, 7.04, 7.04, 7.54, 6.03, 6.53, 7.04, 6.53, 6.03, 6.53, 6.03, 6.53, 6.03, 6.03, 6.53, 7.54, 7.04, 6.03, 6.03, 6.03, 6.53, 6.53, 6.03, 5.03, 4.02, 5.03, 5.03, 4.52, 6.53, 5.53, 8.04, 9.55... 
So early morning values are in the range 4-8. The query day early morning has values around 0-3.41, much lower. So the non-zero segments are also reduced. Attack 2 alone wouldn't explain the reduction in non-zero values. So Attack 2 is not a good match because it only replaces some intervals with zero, not scale the remaining ones.

- Attack 3: multiply each sample by a different random factor between 0 and 1. This would produce varying reductions, could produce very low values but would not produce exact zeros (unless the factor is exactly 0 for some, but description says random factor between 0 and 1, could include 0? Typically "between 0 and 1" means (0,1) open interval? The definition says "by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." If factor can be 0, then zeros possible. But more likely it's (0,1) and zeros not expected. And the query day has multiple zeros exactly, not just near-zero. Attack 3 might produce values near zero if factor is very small, but exact zeros suggest intentional zeroing. Attack 3 could produce zeros if factor exactly 0, but it's less likely. Also, Attack 3 changes trend and amplitude. Could be possible.

- Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. This could produce a segment of reduced values (not necessarily zeros) and normal elsewhere. The query day has a zero block, but also seems overall reduced, not just one period. For example, the max is 7.93, while normal max is ~13.57, so even the highest period is reduced. Attack 4 would typically have a period where values are scaled down or replaced, while other periods are normal. Here, morning values (after zeros) are also low. For instance, reading 31: 2.41, 32:3.41, etc. Normal morning (around 7-8am) values are higher (like 8.04, 9.55). So no normal high values are present. Thus Attack 4 is not fitting because we'd expect some period of unreduced normal consumption.

- Attack 5: Each sample replaced by false value obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by random variable between 0 and 1, different per sample. This would produce a profile with no relation to original, roughly centered around a fraction of the average. The mean of query day is 2.76. Average normal consumption of Day 3 is 7.54. If multiplied by random ~0.36 on average, that could produce mean 2.76. But the query day's values show some pattern: early morning low but not zero except a block, then rise to a peak in the evening, similar shape to normal? Let's compare the shape. Normal Day 3: starts high (midnight to early morning), dips to minimum around 4-5am, rises to morning peak around 8am, then fluctuates, evening peak around 8pm. Query day: starts at 3.41, dips to 0.90, zeros, 0.40, then rises to 2.41, 3.41, then later dip and peak at 7.93 around evening. The shape roughly mimics the normal pattern but scaled down overall and with a zero block. The presence of a zero block is akin to attack 2 or possibly attack IEEE. Attack 5 would produce a completely different pattern random, no resemblance to the normal curve. The query day seems to have a similar diurnal pattern but with reduced magnitude and a stretch of zeros. So Attack 5 unlikely.

- Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. This caps high values. That would reduce high peaks but leave lower values unchanged. In Day 3, min for reference is 4.02, max 13.57. If a cut-off, say 8, is used, then all values above 8 become 8, resulting in max 8, but min still around 4. So mean would be maybe around 5-6. But query min is 0.00, so there are zeros, not just cap. Also, reference Day 3 min is 4.02, so cap wouldn't produce zeros. So Attack 6 cannot produce zeros.

- Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if negative, zero is reported. This shifts the whole profile down by a constant. If cut-off is, say, 6, then values originally 6 become 0, above 6 become reduced. But then original low values (like 4) become 0 as well. This could produce many zeros. Let's test: Day 3 reference min 4.02, max 13.57, mean 7.54. If we subtract a constant C, we can achieve a max of 7.93 and min 0.00. The query max is 7.93, min 0.00. Suppose C = 5.64 (13.57 - 5.64 = 7.93). Then original min 4.02 - 5.64 = -1.62 → 0, so min 0. That would give some zeros (for values <=5.64). Many reference Day 3 values are around 6-8, so they'd become positive but low. The shape would be kept, just shifted down. The query day has a zero block from 5:45 to 6:30. In the reference, what are the values around that time? Day 3 reference: indices 23 (5:45-6:00): 5.03; 24 (6:00-6:15): 4.02; 25 (6:15-6:30): 5.03; 26: 5.03; 27: 4.52. If we subtract ~5.64, all become negative → zero. That matches the zero block. Other low values like reference 6.03 become 0.39 (matches 0.40 observed). Reference 6.53 becomes 0.89 (matches 0.90). So many numbers align. Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." This perfectly explains the query day: a constant shift downward, with negative values clamped to zero. The shift would be a constant C such that max becomes about 7.93. Let's estimate C: original max of Day 3 is 13.57, max of query is 7.93, difference 5.64. Original min 4.02 would become -1.62 → 0, matching min 0. Consistent. The mean: original mean 7.54 - 5.64 = 1.90, but because negative values are zeroed, zeros increase mean slightly? Actually, after clamping, the mean would be higher than 1.90 because zeros are not negative. Let's compute approximate mean: sum(original_i) = 7.54*96 = 723.84. Subtract 5.64*96 = 541.44, sum becomes 182.4. But those original_i < 5.64 become 0, so we add back some amount? Wait, the operation is: new_i = max(0, original_i - C). So the sum is sum_{i} max(0, orig_i - C). That sum is less than sum(orig_i - C) because negatives become 0, adding back the absolute negative. The mean of query is 2.76, much higher than 1.90. So it's plausible with a C around 5.64, many values are just above zero, raising the mean. So Attack 7 fits very well: the pattern is identical to original Day 3 but shifted down and zeroed where negative. And note that Attack 7 description says: "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." Indeed, the shape (trend) is preserved, just shifted. The query day shows a similar trend to Day 3: early morning dip, then rise, then evening peak. The zero block corresponds to the original trough. That's a strong match.

- Attack 9: Flat constant profile. Not the case.

- Attack 10: Reverses the pattern. Not the case.

- Attack 12: Replace with lower-consuming user's pattern. That could produce lower values but would not necessarily preserve the shape of Day 3. It could have a zero block if that user had zero, but likely to have a different pattern. Attack 7 is more consistent.

- Attack IEEE: For each 15-min interval, either reports actual or zero. This would result in a mix of original readings and zeroes. Here, the non-zero readings are not at normal levels; they are reduced. For example, the highest query reading is 7.93, while original max is 13.57. So if it were IEEE attack, the non-zero intervals should show actual consumption (original values). But they are much lower. So IEEE attack would produce readings like: actual, actual, zero, actual, zero, actual, etc., where the actual values match the reference. That's not the case. Thus IEEE is ruled out.

Thus, Attack 7 is the best match.

Now, we must confirm that the flagged day is indeed manipulated. The statistics show a drastic reduction and zeros not seen in the normal week. The normal week shows no zeros at all (mins 3.52-5.53). So it's clearly manipulated. So classification: Manipulated.

Identify attack: Attack 7.

Confidence: High, because the pattern perfectly matches a downward shift with clamping to zero.

Reasoning: The query day's consumption trace closely resembles the reference Day 3's shape but lowered by a constant offset, leading to zero values during low-usage periods (early morning). This aligns with Attack 7's definition. Attack 1 and 3 cannot produce exact zeros; Attack 2 would not reduce non-zero segments; Attack 4 would show a period of reduction but still normal elsewhere; Attack 6 caps but no zeros; Attack IEEE would keep original values in non-zero intervals. So Attack 7 is the only plausible one.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation: A cut-off value (approximately 5.64) is subtracted from the actual consumption of the day. Any sample that would fall below zero is reported as zero. This manipulation reduces the overall magnitude of consumption while preserving the daily trend (shape) of usage. The zeros appear during the morning trough, and all other values are shifted down. Possible technical causes include false data injection or a meter bypass that reports consumption as the difference from a fixed threshold, e.g., by reprogramming the meter to offset consumption.

Classification:

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 7
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day shows a drastic overall reduction in consumption (mean ~7.2 vs normal ~32.5) with multiple zero readings concentrated in a continuous block (indices 13–28) and sporadic zeros later. The non-zero values still follow the typical daily trend (morning hump, midday dip, evening peak) but are uniformly shifted downward by approximately 20–23 units, consistent with subtracting a constant cut‑off and zeroing negative results. Attack 7 uniquely explains both the long zero block (where normal consumption falls below the cut‑off) and the proportionally reduced peaks elsewhere.  
Top alternatives ruled out:  
- Attack 2 (on‑off): produces a zero block but leaves non‑zero intervals at original magnitudes; here the evening peak is only ~29 vs normal ~50+, so the reduction outside the zero block contradicts Attack 2.  
- Attack 3 (random per‑sample scaling): would yield scattered zeroes if some scaling factors are zero, but the occurrence of a 16‑sample contiguous zero block is highly unlikely under independent per‑sample randomisation, and the overall pattern lacks the erratic character expected from varying scaling factors.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A randomly selected constant offset (cut‑off) is subtracted from every 15‑minute reading. Any resulting negative value is replaced by zero. In this case, the subtraction eliminated nearly all consumption during the early‑morning low‑usage period (03:15–07:00), creating a continuous block of zeros, and similarly caused intermittent zeros later when normal usage dipped below the cut‑off. The remaining values are the normal readings minus the offset, preserving the daily profile shape.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Overall magnitude is severely reduced (mean daily consumption drops from ~32.4 to 7.15, maximum from 52–61 to 29.9). The trend (morning rise, midday trough, evening peak) remains intact but is shifted downwards, with low‑consumption intervals flattened to zero.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection, meter bypass, or a measurement offset that effectively subtracts a fixed amount from the true consumption.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Maybe it's Attack 3: each sample multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. That could result in many small values and some zeros if factors are very low? But factors between 0 and 1 multiplied by a positive number would yield values >0 unless the factor is exactly 0. But random continuous factors are unlikely to produce exact zeros. However, if the factor is drawn from a distribution that can be exactly 0, it might. Typically, "random value between (0,1)" implies open interval, so no zero. But the description for Attack 3 says "different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." It doesn't specify open/closed, but usually means (0,1). So exact zeros would be unlikely. Attack 1 multiplies by a constant random factor, so no zeros unless factor is 0 (but it's between (0,1) so not zero). Attack 5 replaces each sample by avg * random(0,1), which would not be zero unless random is zero. So likely the attack involves intentional zero substitution.

Let's reconsider Attack 2: "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" suggests that there is a continuous block of zeros with duration chosen randomly. In the query day, there is a long stretch of zeros from interval 28 onwards, but with occasional non-zero spikes. So it's not a pure contiguous block. Attack IEEE is per-interval random zeroing. But that would require non-zero values to be actual readings. The query day non-zero values are much lower than normal, so not actual.

Maybe there's an attack that combines zeroing and reduction. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off point, and if negative reports zero. That could produce zeros. Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. ... Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." That could yield many zeros if the cut-off is large. For example, if cut-off is 50, then many values become zero because original values are often above? Actually, reference day 4 has min=37.61, so subtracting a cut-off of around 37 would make many zeros. But the query day has non-zero values up to 33.97. So if cut-off is around 30-35, some original high values would become small positive, while lower values become zero. That could mimic the pattern: early morning original values ~80-90, subtract cut-off ~50-60? To get query day early values ~13-16, cut-off would be around 65-75. Then original values around 79-91 subtracted by say 75 yields 4-16, which matches. And original values that are below 75 become zero. In the reference day 4, many daytime values dip to 40-50, so they would become zero. That seems plausible. But Attack 7's description says "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." Subtracting a constant does preserve the shape relative to the threshold. So the trend (shape of peaks) remains if you ignore zeros. The query day shows a morning peak (low), then zeros, then an evening peak. That matches the normal trend: morning peak, midday dip, evening peak. So Attack 7 may fit.

Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." That would cap values, not produce zeros. So not that.

Attack 0: all zeros, not.

Attack 10: reverse pattern, not.

Attack 12: replace with lower-consuming user's pattern, not.

So the main candidates: Attack 2 (zero for random duration), Attack 7 (subtract cut-off, zero if negative), maybe Attack 4 (reduce over period). Attack 4 reduces all measurements in a randomly selected period. The query day shows a period of zeros, but also reduced values outside that period. It could be that the "randomly selected period" covers most of the day, and reduction could be to near-zero or zero. But the definition says "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify how they are reduced; it could be a scaling or subtraction. If they are reduced to zero, that's like zeroing. But Attack 4 typically implies a contiguous interval. The query day has zeros from interval 28 to near the end, but with breaks. Actually, from interval 28 to interval 50-ish, mostly zeros with a couple of small spikes. Then later, from interval 51 onwards, there are some non-zero values and zeros mixed. It's not a single contiguous block. So Attack 4 likely expects a single continuous block of reduction.

Attack IEEE: zero vs actual per interval, but actual values should be normal. Not matching.

Attack 2: single contiguous zero block. Not matching.

Attack 7: subtract cut-off, zeros if negative. This can produce zeros in any interval where original value < cut-off. Since the cut-off can be chosen randomly, the pattern of zeros will correspond to low-consumption times, which typically occur at night and midday. In reference day 4, the lowest values are around 37.61, but many values are in the 40-70 range. If cut-off is chosen such that many values below a certain level become zero, the zeros would appear during low-usage periods. In the query day, zeros appear mostly in the middle of the day and also some at the end? Actually, at the end there are non-zero values (like 15.17, 33.97...). In the reference day 4, the late afternoon/evening has high values (up to 111.20). So if cut-off is, say, 70, then original evening values of 80-111 become 10-41, which matches the query day evening values (e.g., 33.97, 32.34, etc.). The original morning values around 79-91 become 9-21, matching query morning (13-16). The midday values of 40-50 become zero. So Attack 7 fits well.

Let's check Attack 7's description again: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." Yes. The trend (shape of the profile) remains: morning peak, midday dip, evening peak, but with many zero-floor values. The query day shows exactly that: a morning hump (though low), then zeros, then evening hump. So the trend is preserved, just scaled and zeroed. That is consistent.

Could it be Attack 3? Attack 3 multiplies each sample by a random factor between 0 and 1, which would scale each sample differently, potentially preserving the trend shape? Actually, different random factors per sample would distort the shape because each sample is scaled by a different amount, so the relative magnitudes change. If the factors are random, the original trend might be obscured. In the query day, the relative shape of the non-zero portions still resembles the normal pattern: a peak in the morning, then a trough, then a higher peak in the evening. The morning peak in normal day 4 is around 79-91, evening peak higher (100+). In query day, morning peak is ~13-16, evening peak ~33, so the ratio between them is roughly preserved? Let's compute: normal morning max ~91, evening max ~111, ratio 1.22. Query morning max ~16.8, evening max ~33.97, ratio ~2.02. That's not preserved. So the scaling is not uniform across time; morning and evening are scaled differently. But if cut-off is constant, the subtraction would produce a larger relative reduction for lower values. For example, original 90 minus 70 =20, original 110 minus 70 =40, ratio 2. That matches the query ratio. So Attack 7 with subtractive cut-off produces exactly that effect: lower original values become near zero or zero, higher values survive more. This explains the query day's pattern where the evening peak is relatively higher than the morning peak compared to normal. In normal, evening peak is only slightly higher; in query, evening peak is double the morning peak. This is because after subtracting a fixed cut-off, the evening values, being higher, retain more magnitude.

Therefore, Attack 7 is the most likely.

Attack IEEE ruled out because non-zero values are much lower than normal, not actual readings. Attack 7's subtractive cut-off explains the zero floor and the relative preservation of trend with amplified evening peak.

The non‑zero block occurs during the time when the reference week shows a high evening peak; adding back a cutoff ≈460 to the query values reconstructs a plausible evening load curve (e.g., 9.88+460≈470, 158.88+460≈619, etc.) consistent with the customer’s typical peak. Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- The attacker picks a cutoff value (estimated ≈460). For each 15‑minute reading, the cutoff is subtracted from the true consumption. If the result is negative, it is reported as 0.0; otherwise the reduced value is reported. In this query day, the normal base load (around 175–350) and moderate consumption all fall below the cutoff and become zero. Only the highest part of the evening peak, which exceeded the cutoff, survives as a diminished block of readings (max 158.88).  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The total daily consumption is drastically reduced (mean drops from ~348 to 15). The normal diurnal trend is destroyed: the profile is flat zero for most of the day, with only a brief, low‑magnitude bump in the late evening.  
- Possible technical causes: False data injection where the meter firmware or communication is tampered to subtract a threshold, or an intermittent bypass/partial bypass strategy that only reports consumption when it is above a certain level, combined with total bypass during lower‑usage periods.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That might be Attack 4 with a reduction that zeroes out most hours but leaves a block? That seems less likely.

Attack 1: multiplies by constant factor between 0 and 1. That would scale all values, not produce zeros unless factor is 0, but then all would be zero. Not matching.

Attack 3: multiplies by different random factors per sample. That would generate a scaled version of the original, but with varying factors. Since original values are always well above zero (min 175), a factor less than 1 would still give non-zero values unless factor=0 for many samples. It could theoretically produce zeros if the factor for those samples was zero, but that's not typical; Attack 3 usually uses factors in (0,1), meaning non-zero. It could produce zeros if the factor is drawn from a distribution that includes zero, but the description says "random values between 0 and 1", which typically excludes 0. So zeros are unlikely.

Attack 5: replaces with random value based on average normal consumption multiplied by random factor. That would produce values uncorrelated with the actual time pattern, but the query day shows a block of non-zero values that still has some shape (rising and falling), which might be random but could also be a scaled-down version of a part of the day. It's not completely random noise; it has a peak of 158.88 then declines. That could be a scaled version of an evening peak? The original evening peak is very high; scaling by a small factor would give lower values, but then why are other hours exactly zero? That would require the scaling factor for those hours to be zero. So Attack 5 with a per-sample factor that includes zero for many samples, but that's more like Attack 3 with zero factors. Attack 5 uses the average normal consumption as base, so all values would be around that average times random factor; they wouldn't replicate the shape of the load curve. Here the non-zero block has a shape that resembles a consumption pattern (rise and fall), so it might be derived from the actual consumption pattern. That points to scaling of actual values.

Attack 6: cut-off point, values above it replaced by that cut-off. That would cap values, not create zeros. If the cut-off is low, many values become the cut-off, but not zero. For example, if cut-off is 100, all values above 100 become 100, and values below 100 remain (but original values are all >=175, so all would be 100, resulting in constant 100, not a block of varying values and zeros). Not match.

Attack 7: subtract a cut-off; if negative, report zero. For example, if cut-off is 400, then subtract 400 from all values; many become negative -> zero. Some may remain positive. That could produce a pattern where only the highest consumption periods remain non-zero (since original peak >600, minus 400 leaves ~200), while lower consumption periods (e.g., base load ~200) become zero. Let's test: Reference day 4 values range from 182.12 to 619.21. If we subtract, say, 175, then values below 175 become zero, values above 175 become (original - 175). The base load of 182 becomes 7, which is near the 9.88 seen in query day. And peak values become 619-175=444, much higher than the 158 max in query day. But we could subtract a higher value, say 500. Then peak 619-500=119, base 182-500 negative -> zero. That would yield zeros for most of the day, and a small non-zero block around the evening peak. The query day non-zero block is around 19:30-23:00, which aligns with the evening peak. So Attack 7 with a suitably chosen cut-off subtraction could produce this pattern. That seems very plausible. 

Attack 9: flat constant average. Not matching.

Attack 10: reversal, not matching.

Attack 12: replacement with lower-consuming user's pattern. The non-zero block might be from another user? But it's very low and occurs only in the evening; it might be a different pattern. Not impossible, but Attack 7 seems more straightforward.

Attack IEEE: As argued, it implies actual consumption for some intervals, which doesn't match because the non-zero intervals are too low. Unless the "actual electricity consumption" during those intervals happened to be extremely low due to some anomaly, but unlikely given the reference week's consistently high values.

So the most likely attack is Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." This produces a pattern where only the periods with highest consumption (above the cut-off) show non-zero, reduced values, while lower consumption periods become zero. The query day has long zeros and a block of reduced non-zero values that occur during what would be the evening peak. The statistics: min=0, max=158.88, mean=15.13. The non-zero block peaks at 158.88. If we assume the cut-off was around maybe 460? Let's check. On Day 4 reference, values range 182-619. If we subtract, say, 460, then 619-460=159, which matches the max of 158.88 closely! And 582.78-460=122.78, 615.89-460=155.89, 612.58-460=152.58, 619.21-460=159.21, etc. That's very close. The non-zero block in query day: 9.88, 33.06, 42.99, 119.15, 148.95, 158.88, 155.57, 139.02, 132.39, 145.64, 115.84, 105.90, 72.79, 49.61, 23.12. If we add back 460, these become ~470, 493, 503, 579, 609, 618, 615, 599, 592, 605, 575, 565, 532, 509, 483. That's a plausible load curve for the evening: rising to a peak around 618, then declining. The reference day 4 evening values (indices 75-92): 513.25, 523.18, 533.11, 582.78, 582.78, 615.89, 612.58, 619.21, 599.34, 586.09, 579.47, 569.54, 569.54, 569.54, 569.54, 552.98, 543.05, 536.42, etc. Our reconstructed values from query day plus 460 approximately match that pattern: 470, 493, 503, 579, 609, 618, 615, 599, 592, 605, 575, 565, 532, 509, 483. There's one discrepancy: the first non-zero at 9.88 becomes 470, but the actual reference around 19:30 is 582.78. But note that the query day might not be exactly the same day as Day 4 reference week; it's a different Thursday, so consumption pattern may differ somewhat, but the general shape and magnitude are consistent. The cut-off subtraction explains the zeros for all intervals where original consumption <= cut-off. So I'm confident this is Attack 7.

Alternative attacks considered: Attack 2 (on-off) could also produce zeros and non-zero intervals, but the non-zero intervals would be original consumption, not reduced. Since the max of non-zero block is 158.88, far below normal, Attack 2 is ruled out unless the "on" periods were also attacked, but the definition doesn't include reduction during "on" periods. Attack 4 (random period reduced) might reduce values in a selected period, but it doesn't typically produce zeros unless the reduction is total bypass for that period, which would be similar to Attack 2. But Attack 4 reduces measurements, not necessarily to zero. To get zeros, they'd have to be reduced to zero, which could be a special case. However, Attack 7 specifically subtracts a cut-off and produces zeros for values below it, which matches the pattern of a block of reduced non-zero values exactly where the load exceeds the cut-off. The shape of the non-zero block is a scaled-down version of the peak, consistent with subtraction rather than a constant reduction factor (multiplication). So Attack 7 is best.

Thus:
Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack: Most Likely: Attack 7
Confidence: High
Reasoning: The query day shows a long period of zero consumption followed by a short burst of low, non-zero values that correspond to the evening peak consumption period. This pattern matches Attack 7 (subtracting a cut-off point and reporting zero for negative results). The non-zero values, when added with the cut-off, reconstruct a plausible evening consumption curve similar to the reference week's evening peaks. Attack 2 (on-off) was considered but ruled out because the non-zero block values are not original (they are much lower than the minimum reference values). Attack 4 was considered but it does not typically produce zeros unless the reduction is complete, and the shape of the non-zero block aligns better with a subtraction than a multiplicative reduction.
Attack_Behavior_Explanation: The attacker subtracts a fixed cut-off value from each actual consumption reading. If the result is negative, it is replaced by zero. This manipulation eliminates low and moderate consumption periods (reporting them as zero) and only leaves a portion of the highest consumption periods as non-zero but reduced values. In this case, the cut-off appears to be around 460, causing most of the day (base load ~175-350) to become zero, while the evening peak (originally >460) is reduced by about 460, resulting in a block of low readings peaking at 158.88. This changes both the magnitude (drastically reduced total consumption) and the trend (the profile is flattened except for a short evening bump). Possible technical causes include false data injection or a total meter bypass during non-peak periods, combined with partial bypass or manipulation during peak periods, or advanced false data injection that subtracts a threshold.

Thus final answer.

**Classification:** Manipulated

**Identified Attack:**  
- Most Likely: Attack 7  
- Confidence: High  
- Reasoning: The query day’s consumption is drastically reduced compared to the reference Sunday (DAY_ID=0 in the week) – mean drops from ~45.84 to 2.65, and many readings are exactly zero. The non‑zero values appear only in intervals that correspond to the highest-consumption periods of the original day, suggesting a subtractive threshold. This matches Attack 7, where a fixed offset is subtracted from every sample and negative results become zero.  
- Why Attack 7 and not Attack 1: Attack 1 (constant scaling factor) would produce a uniformly scaled version of the original without any exact zeros (original never had zero values).  
- Why Attack 7 and not Attack 2: Attack 2 (on‑off) zeroes only selected periods but leaves the rest at the original values; here the surviving values are far lower than the original (e.g., 4.65 vs. ~46.74), so they have been reduced, not just zeroed.  
- Also Attack 3 was considered but ruled out: random per‑sample scaling factors would break the trend structure, while Attack 7 preserves the daily shape (higher afternoon/evening) and only truncates lower parts.

**Attack_Behavior_Explanation:**  
- How consumption values are manipulated: A fixed offset is subtracted from each original 15‑minute reading. If the result is negative, the reported value is zero. This effectively sets a threshold – only consumption that exceeds the offset is recorded, while all lower usage disappears.  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is severely reduced; many samples become zero, and the remaining non‑zero values are the original consumption minus the offset, so the overall mean falls dramatically. The original trend (e.g., higher values in the afternoon/evening) is preserved because the subtraction and flooring maintain the ordering of the non‑zero points.  
- Possible technical causes: False data injection or total meter bypass where the attacker manipulates the reported readings by always subtracting a chosen amount, claiming zero when actual consumption is below that amount.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 2 only zeros, but this also scales. Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That would be exactly Attack 2: either original or zero. Here, the non-zero values are much lower than original, so it's not just original values. So Attack IEEE is out because it doesn't scale.

Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. The original day 0 values range 39.66-55.24. If a cut-off point is chosen, say 13.15 (the max of query), then all values above 13.15 would be set to 13.15, but values below would remain original. The original values are all > 39, so they would all be cut off to 13.15, resulting in a constant 13.15. That's not what we have. If cut-off is lower, like 13.15, all original values are higher, so they'd all be 13.15, but query has many zeros and small values. If cut-off is 0, it's Attack 0. So not Attack 6.

Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if result negative, zero. So if original values are all 40-55, subtracting a cut-off like 40 would give values 0-15. That could produce zeros when original <= offset. For instance, if offset = 40, then original 39.66 becomes 0, 55.24 becomes 15.24. That could create a pattern where low original values become zero, higher become small positive. Let's test: if offset = 40, original values 39.66->0, 41.08->1.08, 45.33->5.33, 46.74->6.74, 50.99->10.99, 55.24->15.24. The numbers don't align perfectly but might be due to rounding? But the query values are not precise differences, but they are close. Also note that zeros occur where original value minus offset <=0. If offset is 42, then original values <=42 become zero. Original has many values in 39-42 range, so many zeros. Meanwhile, the pattern of non-zero values would follow the shape of original minus offset, preserving trend (since subtraction preserves ordering). That matches the description: "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." So Attack 7 fits the pattern: from a high-level view, the trend (up and down movements) would be preserved because subtracting a constant and flooring at zero keeps the same ordering of non-zero values. In query, we see that the non-zero values appear when original values are higher, and zeros when original lower. And the non-zero values appear in the same relative pattern as the original's higher periods. For example, original has a peak around indices 74 (53.82) and 86 (55.24). In query, we see elevated values around indices 70 (13.15), 75 (10.31), and 91 (11.73). So the peaks align to some extent. So Attack 7 seems very plausible.

Attack 9: flat constant profile equal to average daily consumption. Not this.

Attack 10: reverses the consumption pattern without reducing total amount. Not this; total reduced dramatically.

Attack 12: replaces with pattern of lower-consuming user. Could be any pattern. But not likely given specific structure.

Now, compare Attack 2 vs Attack 7. Attack 2 only zeroes out some periods but leaves the rest original; here non-zero values are not original, so not Attack 2.

Attack 3: scaling with random factor per sample. That would preserve the pattern but with variable scaling, including zeros if factor is zero. While possible, Attack 3 typically uses factors drawn uniformly, so zeros would be rare unless zero is explicitly allowed. But here we see exactly zeros in many places, and the non-zero values seem to correspond to original minus some constant, not a ratio. The values like 4.65 could come from 46.74 * 0.099, but then 11.73 from 48.16 * 0.243; the ratio isn't consistent. The subtraction model yields values proportional to original - offset, which would give a roughly linear relationship with same ordering. In Attack 3, the scaling factors are random and independent, so the ordering of values might not be preserved: a sample that was originally high could become low if its random factor is small, and a low sample could become high if its factor is large. That would break the trend. The description says Attack 3 "Changes both the trend and amplitude." That's consistent with random scaling. Attack 7 says "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." So if the query day's trend (the relative ups and downs) is similar to original, that favors Attack 7 over Attack 3.

Let's compare the trend visually in the numbers. Original day 0: night/morning (0-47) relatively low with minor fluctuations. Afternoon/evening (48-95) slightly higher. Query day non-zero values: early morning have some small non-zero (1.82, 4.65, 3.23, etc.) then many zeros, then a cluster later with higher values (max 13.15) around indices 58-78. That mirrors the original: early morning original is around 40-50, later original is mostly 45-55, so a bit higher. So the later period has higher consumption in original, and query also has higher values there. In between, there are zeros. So the trend (i.e., higher later than earlier) is preserved, albeit with many missing values. Attack 7 with a subtraction offset would produce exactly that: early morning values are lower so many become zero, later values are higher so they remain positive but reduced. In other words, the offset acts as a threshold: only the highest parts of the original profile survive above zero. The original day 0 has a slight upward trend across the day, so the surviving points are more in the later part. So Attack 7 matches very well.

Attack 3 with random scaling factors could also produce a similar pattern if the scaling factors are not truly random but correlated with magnitude? But they are supposed to be unique random per sample, so no guarantee that later samples get larger factors. In fact, random factors would make the pattern erratic and not necessarily preserve the overall daily shape. So Attack 7 is more likely.

Attack 4 would only reduce a contiguous period, but zeros occur throughout, not just one block.

Attack 5 would generate random values around average, which would not correlate with original temporal pattern, but here the late-day peak correlates, so unlikely.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 7.

The non-zero values appear only in periods that correspond to the highest consumption periods of the original day (afternoon/evening), suggesting a subtractive threshold where only values above a cut-off are reported. Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. - Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day's consumption is massively reduced (mean ~8.86 vs reference week mean ~22.6) yet perfectly preserves the temporal consumption pattern and the meter's characteristic step increments (~0.61). This rules out Attack 1 because no single scaling factor can explain the full range (min and max ratios differ substantially). Attack 3 is ruled out because random per‑sample multipliers would disrupt the clear step structure and alter the daily trend, whereas the trend remains intact. Attack 7 perfectly matches: a constant offset subtracted from every sample uniformly lowers the magnitude while leaving the trend untouched.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A fixed cut‑off value (approximately 14) is subtracted from each 15‑minute reading of the original day. All resulting values remain positive, so no zero‑flooring is triggered.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The magnitude of every reading is reduced by a constant amount, significantly lowering daily energy (about 60% reduction). The consumption trend (timing of peaks, troughs, and relative variations) is preserved exactly.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection (meter firmware subtracts a fixed bias before reporting) or a total meter bypass that diverts a constant current, causing a uniform under‑measurement across all intervals.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are given data for a smart meter. Reference week: 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6), each with 96 readings (15-min intervals). Query day (DAY_ID=1) also has 96 readings. We need to determine if the query day is original or manipulated. If manipulated, identify the most likely attack type.

First, examine the reference week statistics: 
Day 0: mean=22.55, min=22.03, max=23.87, std=0.40
Day 1: mean=22.73, min=22.03, max=24.48, std=0.51
Day 2: mean=22.59, min=22.03, max=23.87, std=0.39
Day 3: mean=22.57, min=22.03, max=23.87, std=0.42
Day 4: mean=22.86, min=22.03, max=24.48, std=0.50
Day 5: mean=22.40, min=20.20, max=23.26, std=0.64
Day 6: mean=22.56, min=22.03, max=23.87, std=0.40

The reference week shows consumption values typically in the range around 22-23, with occasional peaks up to 24.48 or dips down to 20.20 on Day 5. The consumption pattern is fairly stable with small variations.

Query day: mean=8.86, min=8.03, max=11.09, std=0.59. The values are much lower overall, roughly around 8-11, compared to ~22-24 in the reference week. The mean of query day is about 39% of the reference means (8.86 / 22.7 ≈ 0.39). This is a drastic reduction. Also, the values are not zero; they are positive but much lower. The query day's std is 0.59, similar to reference days (0.4-0.6), so the variability is similar but scaled down.

Now, let's look at the temporal pattern. Query day is DAY_ID=1, which corresponds to Monday (dow=0) and cal_month=4, dom=8. In the reference week, Day 0 is Sunday (dow=6), Day 1 Monday, etc. So query day is a Monday, just like reference Day 1 (Monday). So we can directly compare with reference Day 1. Reference Day 1 has values typically 22.03-24.48, with mean 22.73. Query day has much lower values. The pattern: Query day starts with 8.64, 9.25, 9.25, 8.64, ... then later around reading index 66-77 (approx 16:00-19:00) we see peaks like 11.09, 10.48, etc. In reference Day 1, the readings also show some variation, with peaks up to 24.48 later in the day. So the query day seems to have a similar temporal shape but scaled down significantly.

The attack types: Attack 1 multiplies by a constant random factor between 0 and 1, so magnitude is reduced but trend is the same. Attack 3 multiplies by different random factors per sample, so trend and amplitude both change. Attack 5 replaces with false values obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by random variable between 0 and 1, producing a completely different pattern. Attack 12: replaces with lower-consuming user's pattern. Attack IEEE: zeroing out some intervals.

Here, the query day values are all positive and lower, with a pattern that looks similar to normal but scaled. The mean ratio is about 0.39. If it were Attack 1 (multiply by constant), the constant would be around 0.39. But is it exactly constant? Check ratios: Reference Day 1 values vs query day values? So the scaling factor varies somewhat. The standard deviation ratio: 0.59/0.51 ≈ 1.16, so the scaled std is actually higher relative to the mean? Actually, std of query is 0.59, while reference is 0.51, so the variability is slightly larger in absolute terms given the lower mean. If it were a constant scaling, the std would be scaled by the same factor, so expected std ≈ 22.73 * factor? Actually, if each value X is multiplied by constant c, then mean = c * mean_ref, std = c * std_ref. So if c ≈ 0.39, std should be about 0.39 * 0.51 ≈ 0.199, but the query std is 0.59, which is much larger. So it's not a constant scaling; the variance is higher relative to the mean. Therefore, the scaling factor varies significantly from sample to sample, making the pattern less smooth.

Confidence is high, because the numbers align perfectly with a constant subtraction. Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A fixed offset (cut-off point) is subtracted from each original consumption sample. Any resulting negative value would be reported as zero, but in this case all original values were above the offset, so the entire profile is shifted downward by about 8.5 units.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is reduced uniformly, lowering total daily consumption by approximately 20%. The trend (shape of consumption over time) remains identical to the normal pattern.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection or a meter bypass that introduces a constant negative bias in reported readings.

- Most Likely: Attack 7  
- Confidence: High  
- Reasoning:  
  The query day's magnitude is uniformly lower than any day in the reference week (mean 24.56 vs 37–39), yet its standard deviation (10.79) is nearly identical to the reference Saturday (11.46) and other weekdays. This indicates that the reduction is a constant offset rather than a multiplicative scaling.
