Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week:  
  A fixed cut‑off amount (approximately 13–14 units) is subtracted from every 15‑minute reading of the original consumption. If a result would fall below zero, zero is reported. This shifts the entire daily profile downward by a nearly constant offset, leaving the shape of the load curve intact.  
- Impact on magnitude and trend:  
  Magnitude is reduced across all hours (e.g., overnight values drop from ~20 to ~8, midday peak from ~56 to ~41). The trend—low overnight, morning ramp‑up, afternoon peak, and evening decline—remains identical to the typical Saturday pattern, because a constant subtraction does not alter relative differences. This is reflected in the nearly unchanged standard deviation (10.79 vs 11.46).  
- Possible technical causes:  
  This attack can be implemented by false data injection or a total meter bypass that artificially offsets the reported consumption by a fixed amount. It may also result from tampering with the metering circuit to introduce a constant bias (e.g., a voltage divider or a shunt) that reduces all readings uniformly.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Which attack type best fits?

Attack 0: all zeros. Not the case, values are non-zero.
Attack 1: multiply by constant factor between 0 and 1. The query day mean is 24.56, reference Saturday mean 38.70, ratio ~0.635. Could be a constant factor ~0.63. So if factor is 0.63, the ratio of each query to reference (if we could align the actual consumption) should be roughly 0.63. But we don't have the true consumption for the query day; we only have the reference Saturday as a baseline. The consumer's behavior on another Saturday could differ, so a perfect constant factor is not expected. But Attack 1 would produce a scaled version of the original consumption, preserving the shape exactly. If we compare query day shape to reference Saturday, do they look similar? Plotting mentally: Reference has overnight ~20-26, then sharp rise to 40s-50s. Query has overnight ~6-12, then rise to 40. But the morning rise in reference starts around index 22-27: from 26.89 to 44.82. In query, from index 22: 14.01, 16.33, 16.33, 18.06, 16.90, 17.48, 29.63 (index 28). So the jump happens around index 28. If scaled, we'd expect query = factor * reference. The shape of scaled reference would have a jump at the same indices. Since they are different days, exact timing of activities may shift, but a constant scaling of one day to produce another is unlikely to match perfectly if the shapes differ. However, the query day does have a similar pattern: low overnight, increase during the day, peak in the afternoon, then decline. But the ratio of peak to overnight min: reference min 19.88, max 56.09, ratio ~2.82; query min 6.42, max 40.89, ratio ~6.37. So the relative amplitude is larger in query day (more dynamic range). That suggests not a simple scaling.

Attack 2: Replaces consumption samples with zeros for a random duration (on-off attack). This would create intervals of zeros, but we don't see zeros in the query day data. The min is 6.42, not zero. So Attack 2 is ruled out.

Attack 3: Multiplies by different random factors between 0 and 1, one per sample. So each sample is scaled by its own factor. That would produce a consumption profile that is like the original but with variable scaling, leading to a noisy version. Could the query day be a noisy scaling of a typical Saturday? If we assume the reference Saturday is the expected consumption, then the query day could be the reference multiplied by random factors around maybe 0.6 with some variance. Let's test crude: For a few samples: If reference sample is 23.41, query same index 12.85, factor = 0.549; 25.73 -> 11.69, factor 0.454; 22.19 -> 8.74, factor 0.394; 26.31 -> 12.27, factor 0.466; 22.77 -> 9.96, factor 0.437; 21.04 -> 8.16, factor 0.388; 19.88 -> 9.31? Wait, index 6: ref 19.88, query 9.31? Actually query index 6 is 9.31, ref index 6 is 19.88, factor 0.468; ... The factors vary quite a bit, from 0.32 to 0.73. So it could be Attack 3, where each sample is multiplied by a random factor.

Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period. All measurements in that period are reduced. So a contiguous block of time is reduced (presumably by a factor). Could be that a part of the day is heavily reduced while other parts are normal. Looking at query day, the entire day's values seem low compared to reference Saturday, not just a period. For example, overnight values are 6-12 vs reference 19-22, so about half; mid-morning to afternoon: query 29-40 vs reference 44-56, so also reduced. There isn't a clear contiguous block that is normal while others are zero or heavily reduced. So Attack 4 is less likely.

Attack 5: Each sample replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with different variable per sample. So the values are random around the average consumption. The query day has a pattern: low at night, high during day, which resembles a typical consumption pattern, not just random noise around a constant average. So not Attack 5.

Attack 6: Cut-off: select a cut-off point and replace values above it with that cut-off. If there's a cap, we would see a flat top at the cut-off. In query day, the max is 40.89, and several values are 40.89 (at indices 45,46,48). Also 40.89 appears three times: 45,46,48. However, 40.89 is not exactly repeated many times; there are also 39.73, 39.15, etc. But there might be a cap around 40.89. Let's check: values above 40? 40.89 appears three times, and there is 39.73, 39.15, 38.57, etc. The reference day has values up to 56.09. If the attacker caps at ~40.89, then many high values would be clipped to that value, creating a plateau. Indeed, query day shows a brief plateau at 40.89 for three consecutive points (45,46,48 with 39.73 at 47). That might be a slight variation, but it's almost a plateau. Also earlier, 37.40 appears twice. But reference day has a sharp peak. Could be Attack 6: the attacker sets a cut-off at 40.89, and any consumption above that is replaced by 40.89. That would explain the plateau and the overall reduction because all high values are capped, reducing the mean. But also note that the entire daily profile is lower, including overnight. Attack 6 only affects values above the cut-off; values below are unchanged. So if the cut-off is 40.89, overnight values should be the same as the original. But reference Saturday overnight values are ~19-26, while query overnight values are ~6-12. That is much lower, not unchanged. So Attack 6 alone can't explain the low overnight values. Unless the attacker combined a cut-off with something else, but we must pick one attack type. So not Attack 6.

Attack 7: A random cut-off point subtracted from each sample; if negative, report zero. So if cut-off is X, then new_value = max(0, original - X). This would shift the whole curve down by X, zeroing values below X. If we set X approximately so that overnight values become ~8-12 instead of ~20, say X ~12. Then original 20 becomes 8, 22 becomes 10, etc. High values of 56-12=44. Query max is 40.89, so that roughly matches. Let's test: reference overnight around 20 - 12 = 8, query overnight around 6-12, close. Reference peak 56 - 12 = 44, query peak 40.89, a bit lower but could be variation. The shape would be preserved exactly (just shifted down and zeroed for negatives). However, note that reference min is 19.88, so after subtracting 12, min becomes 7.88, which is above zero, so no zeroing. Query min is 6.42, which is a bit lower. So if X is around 13-14, then min would be ~6.88-5.88, matching 6.42. But then peak 56-13=43, still close to 40.89. The query day does not have any zeros, so X is not so large that negatives occur. This would be a uniform reduction: each sample reduced by a constant amount (subtractive). Attack 7 is exactly that: a cut-off point is subtracted from each sample, with zero floor. So Attack 7 fits well: the entire profile is shifted down, preserving the trend but reducing magnitude. The trend (pattern of rises and falls) would remain similar because subtraction doesn't alter the relative differences. That matches: query day pattern is similar in shape to reference Saturday (low at night, increase, peak, decrease) but magnitudes are lower. The standard deviation: reference std 11.46, query std 10.79, very close, indicating similar variability. If it were multiplicative scaling, the std would scale by factor (0.63 of 11.46 = 7.22, but query std is 10.79, which is almost the same as reference, suggesting an additive shift (since adding or subtracting a constant does not change standard deviation). Indeed, subtracting a constant from each value leaves the std unchanged. Here query std 10.79 vs ref std 11.46, close, especially considering it's a different day with natural variation. So this strongly points to Attack 7.

Attack 9: All samples replaced by average daily energy consumption (flat constant). Not the case.

Attack 10: Reverses consumption pattern. Not the case, pattern is similar, not reversed.

Attack 12: Replaces with pattern of a lower-consuming user. Could be, but hard to distinguish from Attack 7 without more specifics. Attack 12 would involve entirely different consumption habits. While here the pattern closely resembles the reference Saturday but lower, which is more consistent with an attack on the meter rather than identity theft. But both possible. However, Attack 7 exactly describes a subtractive constant, which matches the preserved std and lowered magnitude. Attack 12 would likely have a different shape (different user's pattern), but here the shape is similar to the reference Saturday. For example, the timing of the morning rise matches. So Attack 7 is more plausible.

Attack IEEE: For each 15-min interval, either reports actual or zero. That would give zeros interleaved with normal values. Not the case.

So most likely Attack 7.

Let's rule out Attack 1 (constant multiplier): Ratio of means is 0.635, but std of query is 10.79 vs reference 11.46, ratio 0.94, not 0.635. If it were scaling, std would also scale. So unlikely.

Attack 3 (random per-sample scaling): That would produce a different std, likely lower and noisier, but here std is similar. Also, a per-sample random scaling would introduce random variation that would distort the smoothness, but the query day seems relatively smooth. For example, neighboring readings often similar (e.g., 40.89, 40.89, 39.73, 40.89). In random scaling, you'd expect more jitter. So Attack 7's subtractive constant better explains the preserved variance.

Thus, I'll classify as Manipulated, Attack 7 with high confidence.

Now, output format: Classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Most Likely: Attack 7. Confidence: High. Reasoning: Compare to Attack 1 and Attack 3, explain why ruled out. Provide Attack_Behavior_Explanation.

- A cut-off point (estimated around 155) is subtracted from each 15‑minute reading. Where the result is negative, the reading is reported as zero. All remaining positive readings are reduced by that cut-off amount.  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Overall consumption magnitude is reduced, and low‑consumption periods become zero. The underlying trend is preserved for periods where consumption exceeds the cut-off, but the amplitude is compressed.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 2 (on-off) produces zeros for a random duration, but the non-zero periods are unchanged. Let's examine Attack 7 description: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." Actually, reading: "replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point" is for Attack 6? Wait: Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." So it caps high values. Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That phrasing is confusing: "replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point" might mean that if the result is negative, zero is reported, effectively replacing values that exceed the cut-off point? Actually, subtracting a cut-off: if you subtract C, then original values > C become positive, values < C become negative -> zero. So the "exceeding" part is not exactly; it's that values that are too low become zero. The description says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That seems contradictory. I think they mean: Attack 6 replaces high values with a cap; Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off, and if negative, reports zero. That is an additive cut-off. So it reduces overall consumption, keeps trend for values above cut-off, zero for low values. That matches the query day's pattern: zeros in low-consumption periods, reduced but non-zero in higher periods. So Attack 7 is a strong candidate.

Now, which one is more plausible given the definitions? Let's see the specific Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude." This could also produce an irregular pattern with zeros interleaved. But the query day has long contiguous zero blocks, not irregular single intervals. So it's less likely.

Attack 2 is "on-off" meaning a random duration of zeros, but the remaining consumption is actual. The query day's non-zero values are not at the typical level; they are much lower. However, note that the reference week days vary; the query day is a different Monday, and the customer's consumption could be lower for that specific day. The reference week's Day 1 had mean 203.34, max 294. The overall reference week days have means ~218-238, max around 294-336. The query day's mean of non-zero values? A typical Monday might have variation, but a drop from >200 to <80 mean is extreme. So it's not just natural variation. So the whole day appears manipulated, not just zero periods.

Attack 7 would produce zeros for periods where original consumption is below the cut-off, and reduced values for above. If we reverse engineer: Suppose cut-off = C. Then query value q = max(0, orig - C). If we assume the reference Monday as typical orig for that day, we could solve for C. For the peak: orig max = 294, q max = 139, so C = 294 - 139 = 155. For night minimum: orig min = 131.81, q min = 0, so C >= 131.81. So C around 155 is consistent: night minimum 131.81 < 155 -> zero; early morning values ~146-150 are <155 -> zero; values above 155 get reduced. That perfectly explains the zero block around indices 14-23: the original morning values might have been around 140-150, below 155 -> zero. The later rise: original values above 155 appear. Then the evening decline: original values fall below 155 again -> zero. So Attack 7 fits the data extremely well.

Attack 3 (random scaling per sample) could also produce low values and possibly zeros if scaling factor is near zero, but not the specific zero blocks in contiguous fashion. Attack 4 (reduce over a period) might not produce zeros. Attack 5 (white noise) no.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 7. I'll reason carefully: The query day shows a pattern where low-consumption periods (night/morning) are zero, while daytime consumption is reduced but retains the overall shape of a typical day. This matches Attack 7, where a cut-off value is subtracted, resulting in negative values becoming zero. The cut-off is likely around 155 based on comparison with reference week. The alternative Attack 2 (on-off) was ruled out because it would preserve actual consumption during non-zero periods, which here are significantly lower than the reference week, and the zero blocks align with low-consumption times rather than random durations. Attack IEEE was ruled out because zeros appear in contiguous blocks, not as per-interval random substitutions.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A fixed cutoff value (estimated ~20–25) is subtracted from each 15‑minute consumption reading; any result below zero is reported as zero. This systematically lowers the entire daily profile by a nearly constant amount.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The magnitude is reduced overall, with the effect most pronounced during low‑usage periods (night baseload is cut by 50–75%) and less severe at the daily peak (reduction of ~15–20%). Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Query day indices 0-27: [15.52, 18.50, 21.49, 10.53, 19.50, 20.49, 32.44, 15.52, 18.50, 17.51, 21.49, 15.52, 14.52, 16.51, 17.51, 16.51, 17.51, 21.49, 21.49, 19.50, 13.52, 11.53, 13.52, 13.52, 12.52, 8.54, 10.53, 13.52]

So query day's early morning values are drastically lower, often less than half of reference. Reference Day 1 indices 28 onward: [40.49, 45.46, 82.53, 80.53, 87.52, 93.51,...] Query day indices 28 onward: [38.58, 42.58, 59.56, 56.56, 53.57, 56.56,...] So the rise is less pronounced and the peak values are around 70-80s instead of 90-110s.

Could this be Attack 1 (multiply by constant random factor)? If factor constant across all samples, then each query reading would be roughly factor*reference reading (with possible noise). Let's check some points: reference index 0: 40.49, query index 0: 15.52, ratio 0.383. index 1: 44.47 vs 18.50 ratio 0.416. index 2: 41.48 vs 21.49 ratio 0.518. Not constant. So not Attack 1.

Attack 3: different random factors per sample. That could produce varying ratios, but the pattern of reduction might be similar. However, Attack 3 multiplies by random factor between 0 and 1 each sample, so it would preserve the shape's relative variations but with scaling noise. Here, the early morning ratios are much lower (0.3-0.5) while later ratios are higher. For instance, reference Day 1 index 30: 82.53, query index 30: 59.56 ratio 0.722. Index 40: reference Day 1 index 40? Let's align indices: We need to compare same time of day. Since both are 96 samples, we can directly compare index-wise for the same time. Reference Day 1 index 32: 93.51, query index 32: 53.57 ratio 0.573. So it's not uniform scaling, so not a simple multiplicative attack with constant factor per sample? Actually, Attack 3 says "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." That would mean each sample gets its own random factor. So the query day would be original * random_i, with random_i drawn independently. That would produce a noisy version of the original shape, but the trend (ups and downs) would still be correlated. Here, the query day's shape still has a morning rise and evening drop, but the early morning values are exceptionally low compared to the peak values, suggesting that the scaling factor is not just random around a mean but is systematically lower during low-consumption periods. That points to a possible subtractive or cutoff attack rather than multiplicative.

Attack 6: cutoff - replace values above a threshold with that threshold. That would flatten peaks. Query day max is 101, reference max is higher, but not all values above that are set to 101; we see some values like 83, 81, 77, etc. So not a strict cutoff.

Attack 7: subtract a cutoff point from each sample, if negative report zero. This would reduce all values by a constant amount. If we assume a subtracted constant C, then query = max(0, original - C). For early morning when original is low (30-40), subtracting C would give low numbers or zero. For peak times when original is high (80-110), subtracting C would give 80-C to 110-C. If we try to find C: For index 0, original 40.49, query 15.52 => C ~ 25. Index 1: original 44.47, query 18.50 => C ~ 26. Index 2: 41.48 to 21.49 => C ~ 20. Not consistent. But maybe the original day is not exactly Day 1; we are comparing to a specific reference day, but the query day might have its own inherent variation. However, the query day's values are never zero (min 8.54). So no zero values. Attack 7 says "if the result is negative, zero is reported" but we have no zeros, so maybe C is small? But then it wouldn't reduce so much.

Attack 2: on-off attack where samples are replaced with zeros for random durations. Here no zeros, so not Attack 2.

Attack 0: all zeros, no.

Attack 4: simulated theft over a randomly selected period, all measurements in that period are reduced. That could be a period where values are lowered, e.g., during peak hours or off-peak. Observing query day, the entire day's values seem lower, not just a specific period. So not Attack 4.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by random value between 0 and 1 times average normal consumption. That would produce a completely different pattern, not correlated with the typical time-of-day shape. But query day still shows a rise and fall pattern, so not Attack 5.

Attack 9: all samples replaced by average daily energy consumption, flat constant profile. Not flat, so no.

Attack 10: reverses pattern, no reversal here.

Attack 12: replaces with lower-consuming user's pattern. Could be possible? The pattern might resemble a lower-consuming user but still similar daily shape. This attack replaces the entire consumption pattern with that of another user. The result would look like a normal consumption pattern but lower magnitude. Here, the query day looks like a plausible daily pattern but with much lower morning baseline and less pronounced peak. It could be that of a user who consumes less. But we need to see if the pattern is exactly that of another user or just scaled. Since we don't have other users, we have to infer from the fact that the query day is for the same meter. Attack 12 would be hard to detect without knowing other users. However, we might consider the possibility that the consumption pattern is consistent with a lower-usage day? But the task says "compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation." If the day is consistent (e.g., a lower usage day but still within normal variation), we classify as original. But here the min is 8.54, which is much lower than any reference day min (lowest 32.52). That is a strong indicator of manipulation, because it's unlikely for the same household to have such a dramatic drop in minimum consumption overnight. Also, the pattern's shape: early morning values are extremely low (8-20s). In reference week, early morning values never go below 32. So this is a clear anomaly.

Thus, classification: Manipulated.

Now identify attack type. The early morning values are dramatically lower, while peak values are only moderately reduced. This looks like a "partial reduction" attack where the reduction is more pronounced when consumption is low, possibly due to a threshold or subtraction. But again, subtraction with constant C doesn't perfectly fit because the ratios vary. Alternatively, it could be Attack 3 (different random factors per sample) but the randomness may not be uniform; however, Attack 3 definition says "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." That means each sample is multiplied by an independent random number. In that case, the expected value of query at time t is original(t) * E[factor], and variance would be high. The query day's early morning values are extremely low, which could happen if the random factors for those times happened to be very small. But the pattern seems too systematic: all early morning values are very low, and there's a gradual increase later. Random factors would be independent and would not show such a smooth transition from low factors to higher factors. So Attack 3 is unlikely.

Attack 1 is constant factor, ruled out.

Attack 4: theft over a specific period. But the reduction seems present throughout the whole day, not just a period. For example, late evening (indices ~80-95) also reduced: query last indices 48.57, 46.57, 33.44, etc. Compare reference Day 1 last indices: 77.53, 69.54, 62.40, 57.41, 60.40, 58.41, 64.56, 61.40, 60.40, 62.40, 57.41. So reference last values are in 50s-60s, query are 48.57, 46.57, 33.44,... So still reduced. So it's entire day reduction.

Attack 6 (cutoff) not match because peaks are not capped uniformly.

Attack 7 (subtract then zero if negative) could produce some very low values if C is around 20-30. For original around 32-40, subtracting 20-30 gives 2-20, not zero. For example, original min 32.52, subtract 25 gives 7.52, which is close to query min 8.54. So if C ~ 24, then original 32.52 -> 8.52, original 34.51 -> 10.51. So early morning low values from original 34-40 would map to 10-16, matching query. And peak original 117 -> 93, query max 101, close. So Attack 7 could be a possibility: subtract a constant amount from each sample. Attack 7 description: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." So it's a constant subtraction (not necessarily random? "A random cut-off point" means a threshold chosen randomly maybe once for the attack?). The description says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." Actually wording: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That sounds like Attack 6 uses a cutoff above which values are replaced by the cutoff, while Attack 7 subtracts a constant value, and if negative -> zero. That would uniformly shift the entire profile down by a constant. This would reduce all loads by the same absolute amount, so low loads are reduced proportionally more. This matches the observation: low consumption periods are reduced drastically, while high peaks are reduced moderately. For instance, a baseline of 40 becomes 15 (62% reduction), while 100 becomes 75 (25% reduction). So this looks plausible.

But let's check Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." There are no zeros in the query day, so not IEEE.

Attack 12: replacing with lower-consuming user's pattern. Could be. But Attack 12 description: "The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user." That would likely result in a pattern that looks like another normal user. The query day pattern has a very low baseline (8-15) which is implausibly low for a residential user, given that even at night there is usually some base load (fridge, etc.) of maybe 30-50 units. So it might still be an anomalous pattern rather than a legitimate lower-user. However, Attack 12 is hard to rule out without other users' data. But given the dramatic drop in baseload, it's more indicative of manipulation.

Attack 4: maybe the theft applies to the entire day? Attack 4 says "simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't say reduced by a constant or factor; it might be ambiguous. But the key is "within a randomly selected period". Here, the reduction seems to affect the entire day, not a period. So not Attack 4.

Thus, Attack 7 (constant subtraction) seems the best fit. Let's verify with some calculations: Take reference Day 1 values and subtract, say, 24. Then for a sample of 40.49, result 16.49 (query 15.52). For 44.47, 20.47 (query 18.50). For 41.48, 17.48 (query 21.49 - discrepancy). Not perfect. What if we use a different reference day? The query day might not exactly match any single reference day; it's not the same day, so we shouldn't expect exact match. But the principle of a constant shift fits the pattern of more reduction at low loads. Also, Attack 7 description says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That's confusing: "replaces exceeding values with zero" might mean if the subtraction results in negative, it's set to zero. So it's essentially subtracting a threshold C from all readings, with a floor of zero. That's exactly what we hypothesized.

Now consider alternative attacks: Could it be Attack 3 (varying multiplicative factors) that happen to be low in early morning? Possibly but unlikely because the randomness would produce irregularities, but the query day still shows a smooth pattern. There's no sudden jumps or noise; readings gradually increase and decrease. So multiplicative random factors would create a noisier version, but here the shape remains smooth (just shifted down). So Attack 7 is better.

Also note Attack 4 could be "all measurements in that period are reduced" but if period is the entire day, then the whole day is reduced. However, the definition says "over a randomly selected period", implying not the entire day necessarily. So not typical.

Thus, I'll go with Attack 7.

Confidence: Medium, because Attack 7 explains the pattern well, but Attack 3 could also produce such a shape if factors are correlated (but they are supposed to be independent). Given the smoothness, I'm leaning Attack 7.

Now craft the output:

This is consistent with Attack 7, where a constant offset is subtracted from each reading. This turns all low‑usage periods (night and early morning) into zeros, while the higher‑usage periods are reduced by the same constant amount.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Actually, it says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That suggests that in Attack 7, values that exceed the cut-off are replaced with zero? That would be odd. Probably the definition is: Attack 6 caps at a threshold (values above become threshold). Attack 7 subtracts a threshold, and if the result is negative, zero is reported. So for Attack 7, values above the cut-off are reduced by subtracting, not replaced with zero. The wording "replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point" might be a mistake; I think it means: instead of replacing values above with the cut-off (like Attack 6), Attack 7 subtracts the cut-off, and if the result is negative, it sets to zero. So "exceeding values" might refer to values that are less than cut-off? No. Likely the intended meaning: Attack 7: subtract a cut-off point from each sample; if the result is negative, report zero. This effectively zeros out consumption below the cut-off and reduces consumption above it by the cut-off amount. Yes, that's a common attack type (like "subtract a constant"). The description "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point" is unclear, but I'll go with the former understanding: it's subtraction with flooring at zero.

Given that, Attack 7 perfectly matches: a constant cut-off subtracted, causing zeros for low values, and reduced positive values. This explains the contiguous zero block (during low consumption overnight and early morning) and the reduced magnitude later. The trend of the positive part should roughly follow the original shape, just shifted down. The query day's pattern does look like a scaled-down version of a typical Friday, but with a sharp cut-off between zero and non-zero. So Attack 7 is a strong candidate.

Alternative candidates: Attack 2 (on-off) would leave the non-zero part unchanged, but it is clearly reduced, so not that. Attack 4 (reduction over a period) would reduce values but not necessarily zero them; zeros could appear if factor is zero, but it says reduced, not zeroed. Attack 4 might be a scaling down, not constant subtraction. Attack 3 scales with varying factors, which would produce a similar shape but scaled down, not a constant shift and zeros. That could also explain the zero block only if factors are zero for some samples, but the attack says factors between 0 and 1, not zero exactly. However, it's plausible that a random factor could be zero if the range is inclusive [0,1). But the description says "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1", typically meaning (0,1) open interval. So zero is unlikely. So Attack 3 is less likely.

Attack IEEE: random intervals of zero, but the zeros here are all concentrated in a single block, not scattered. So that's less likely.

So I'd say Attack 7 is the most likely.

Now, confidence: The match is very good because the zero block corresponds to the period when normal consumption is below a threshold, and the non-zero values resemble a shifted version of reference day. However, we don't have the exact original day's data, only reference week. So confidence could be High or Medium. I think High.

Reasoning: Attack 7 subtracts a constant, causing zeros for low periods, which is exactly what we see. Attack 2 (on-off) would leave later values unchanged, ruled out. Attack 4 (period reduction) would scale, not shift by constant, and wouldn't produce zeros unless factor 0, but description says "reduced", not "zeroed". Attack 3 and 1 would not create zeros. Attack 5 (random values based on average) would not resemble original profile at all. Attack 6 caps values, doesn't zero. Attack 9 constant flat, Attack 10 reversal, Attack 12 substitution. So Attack 7 is the only one that creates zeros for low values and subtracts constant.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: A fixed cut-off value (likely around 750-800) is subtracted from each original reading; any resulting negative value is reported as zero. This creates a zero block during low-consumption hours and reduced positive readings during higher-consumption hours.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The magnitude is drastically reduced across the day, with many intervals zeroed and peak values roughly halved. The trend shape above the cut-off is preserved but shifted downward, so the overall pattern still resembles the original load curve in shape but at a lower level.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection or meter bypass techniques that manipulate the reported consumption by subtracting a constant offset; could also be caused by a total meter bypass combined with a partial recording scheme.

Where the original value is below the cut-off, the result is negative and is reported as zero. Where the original value exceeds the cut-off, the reported value is the original minus the cut-off.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Overall consumption magnitude is reduced by the constant cut-off, and many intervals become zero. The underlying trend (e.g., overnight lows, daytime peaks) is preserved, but the lowest portions are flattened to zero.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection or a total meter bypass that under-reports usage by a fixed offset, causing the meter to register zero during low-usage periods and under-report during higher usage.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.

Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. Now, check Attack 4: simulates theft over a randomly selected period where all measurements are reduced. That could give a period of reduced values but not necessarily zeros; it says "reduced" without specifying to zero. Could be scaling. But the description: "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't say reduced to zero. Attack 6 and 7 involve cut-offs. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off point and floors to zero; that could produce many zeros if cut-off is high, but would also produce non-zero values that are original minus constant. In this case, the non-zero values max 37, so if cut-off was, say, around 80-100, many would be zero, and only the highest consumption periods would remain non-zero. But then the non-zero pattern would be the top of the normal profile, which would be around 100+ minus cut-off = maybe 20-30. That could match. For example, if normal daytime peak is ~115, subtract ~80 yields ~35. But the pattern: on reference day 2, the peak hours (e.g., 96, 94, 103, etc.) are high. If cut-off=80, then 96-80=16, 103-80=23, which is in the range. So Attack 7 could produce many zeros and some reduced values. Let's examine query day's non-zero sequence: 0.43, 16.13, 11.84, 6.85, 8.99, 17.74, 12.92, 11.31, 19.34, 30.07, 18.28, 29.53, 28.46, 25.24, 17.20, 23.64, 25.24, 22.03, 26.31, 28.99, 31.68, 33.82, 27.39, 23.64, 25.78, 37.04, 25.24, 27.39, 24.17, 24.71, 18.81, 19.88, 22.03, 30.07, 21.49, 31.68, 21.49, 28.99, 23.10, 28.99, 23.64, 22.56, 19.88, 25.24, 22.56, 20.95, 24.17, 20.95, 17.20. Then after that, some zeros then a few non-zero: 3.64, 0.43, 5.78, then zeros. These non-zero values are all positive, many of them roughly in the range 10-35. Could that correspond to original consumption minus a fixed cut-off? Let's try to approximate. For a given cut-off C, we'd have reported = max(0, original - C). For times with original > C, reported = original - C. So if we see reported values around 20-30, original would have been C+20 to C+30. On day 2 reference, the highest values are around 116. So if C was around 80-90, then original - C would be 20-30. That is plausible. Additionally, the overnight hours typically have original values around 30-60. If C is high, many would go to zero because original < C. Indeed, on reference day 2, nighttime values are around 30-60. So if C is, say, 40, then many would become zero or small. But the evening zeros after the block of non-zero: the reference day's later evening drops to 60s-50s, which might still be > C? Let's check: day 2 reference: after index 80 (20:00), values are around 53-81. So if C is, say, 50, then some would be zero (53-50=3) and others would be non-zero (81-50=31). The query day after the main block has zeros, then a few small non-zero like 3.64, 0.43, 5.78, then zeros. That matches if C is around 50, then original values just above 50 become small numbers, and those below become zero. So Attack 7 fits very well: subtract a constant cut-off (around 50-55) from each sample, report zero if negative. That would eliminate the trend but reduce magnitude, and the statement says "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." Actually, Attack 7 description: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. ... Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." The trend might be somewhat preserved for values above the cut-off, but many become zero, so overall pattern is severely altered. Still, that's what they wrote.

Attack 2 (on-off with zeros for random duration) would not explain the intermediate non-zero values. They'd either be actual values (unlikely given low max) or some manipulated values. But if the "on" periods reported actual consumption, we'd see values similar to normal. Here the max 37 is far below normal peaks. So Attack 2 is less plausible unless combined with scaling, but attack types are singular.

Attack IEEE: "substitutes it with zero or reports actual" - again, actual would be high. So not matching.

Attack 4: "All measurements in that period are reduced." Could be scaling, not subtraction, but "reduced" is vague. If reduced by a constant factor, then no zeros. If reduced by a constant offset, similar to Attack 7. Attack 6 is cut-off ceiling, not subtraction. Attack 7 explicitly uses subtraction.

Thus, Attack 7 is the most consistent because the non-zero values look like a reduced version of the upper part of the normal daily profile, and zeros correspond to times when original consumption was below the cut-off.

Classification: Manipulated

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 7
- Confidence: Medium
- Reasoning: The query day’s mean consumption (75.70) is about half of the reference Saturday’s mean (151.66), yet the standard deviation is identical (31.45). Attack 7 subtracts a constant cut‑off from every sample, which perfectly preserves the standard deviation while lowering the mean. Attack 1 would have scaled both mean and standard deviation by the same factor (not the case). Attack 6 would have reduced the standard deviation by capping high values. Attack 3 (per‑sample random scaling) would almost certainly change the standard deviation and the overall pattern shape. Although the per‑sample differences relative to the reference Saturday are not perfectly constant, this can be attributed to normal week‑to‑week variation in the underlying baseline consumption.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A fixed cut‑off value (approximately 76 units) is subtracted from every 15‑minute reading. If the result is negative, it is reported as zero (no zeroes observed here, indicating the cut‑off was below the minimum of the original day’s consumption).
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is uniformly reduced (mean drops by ∼50%), while the trend (the shape and timing of consumption peaks/troughs) is preserved unchanged because the same constant is subtracted from all values. The standard deviation remains exactly the same as the original day.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection that applies a constant offset to reported measurements, or a partial meter bypass that creates a fixed under‑registration of current.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Let's calculate what would happen to std under independent random scaling: If X_i = a_i * Y_i where a_i are i.i.d. with mean m and variance v, and independent of Y_i, then Var(X) = E[a^2] E[Y^2] - (E[a] E[Y])^2 = (m^2 + v) (Var(Y)+E[Y]^2) - m^2 E[Y]^2 = m^2 Var(Y) + v Var(Y) + v E[Y]^2. So Var(X) = (m^2+v) Var(Y) + v E[Y]^2. For Var(X) to equal Var(Y), we need (m^2+v-1) Var(Y) + v E[Y]^2 = 0. Since all terms positive unless m^2+v<1. With E[Y] around 151, Var(Y)=31.45^2=989.1. Even small v would make the second term large. So it's very unlikely that independent random scaling would preserve the variance exactly. It would almost certainly change it.
