  - Attack 2 (on‑off) was ruled out because its non‑zero intervals would retain the original magnitude, but here the positive readings are far below normal.
  - Attack IEEE (actual‑or‑zero) was ruled out for the same reason – the non‑zero values should match the real consumption, yet they are strongly reduced.

  - Other attacks (scaling, capping, etc.) fail to produce long contiguous zero blocks with simultaneous reduced peaks.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A random cut-off point \(C\) (estimated around 80 – 85) is subtracted from each 15‑minute reading; if the result is negative, the reported value becomes zero. Hence, intervals where typical usage falls below \(C\) (night, early morning, late evening) are zeroed out, while the midday/peak usage is reported as \(\max(0,\, \text{original} - C)\), yielding reduced positive numbers.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude is drastically reduced (daily mean drops from ~83 to 7.38). The underlying trend of diurnal consumption is preserved – a morning ramp‑up, midday peak, and evening decline – but compressed into a much lower range (0–25.5).
- Possible technical causes: False data injection into the meter’s reporting stream; a manipulated firmware that applies a constant bias and floor function; or a partial meter bypass combined with a threshold setting that suppresses low‑level readings.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. 3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Check other attacks:
- Attack 0: all zeros (not).
- Attack 1: constant scaling, would not produce zeros.
- Attack 2: on-off zeros, but non-zero values would be normal magnitude, not reduced.

- Attack 10: reverse pattern (not).
- Attack 12: different user pattern (no zeros).

Classification:

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 7
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day shows a continuous block of zeros from midnight to early morning and again late evening to midnight, while daytime consumption follows a diurnal pattern similar to the reference week but at reduced levels. This matches Attack 7, where a fixed threshold is subtracted from all readings and any negative result is set to zero. Attack 2 (on‑off) was ruled out because the zero periods are not random—they align with low‑usage night hours—and the non‑zero values are significantly lower than typical, not unmodified. Attack IEEE was ruled out because its non‑zero intervals would report actual consumption, but here daytime values are uniformly reduced (e.g., peak ~111 vs. expected >200), not alternating between real and zero.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A constant cut‑off threshold is subtracted from every original 15‑minute sample; whenever the result is negative, the reported consumption becomes zero. As a result, nighttime consumption (normally ~80–120 units) falls below the threshold and is reported as zero, while daytime consumption is reduced by the threshold amount, preserving the shape of the daily profile.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Total daily consumption is drastically lowered (mean drops from ~152 to 39), and the trend is partially altered—the nighttime segments are flat zero, but the daytime ramp‑up and ramp‑down cycles are retained with reduced amplitude.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection or a meter bypass that applies a constant offset to all readings, possibly combined with a “zero floor” routine in the reporting software.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Actually reading carefully: Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." So it caps above. Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." That's not similar to Attack 6 in replacing exceeding values with zero; it's subtract threshold, and negative -> zero. So it's a subtraction, not capping. The "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point" might refer to Attack 7 being like Attack 6 but instead of capping at cut-off, it subtracts cut-off, effectively setting to zero if below, but that doesn't "replace exceeding values" - that wording is off. Actually, the intended Attack 7 might be: you subtract a constant from all values, and negative values become zero. That would not cap high values, just reduce them. The description "replaces exceeding values with zero" might mean if value exceeds something? No, it says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That is confusing. Reread: "Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." Possibly the meaning: In Attack 6, you cap high values at a cutoff. In Attack 7, you subtract the cutoff from all, and for those that become negative, you set to zero. It doesn't "replace exceeding values with zero" - maybe they meant "replaces subceeding values" or something. But given typical smart grid NTL attacks, Attack 7 often refers to a thresholding attack where consumption below a threshold is zeroed, and above is reduced by that threshold amount. So effectively, new = max(0, original - threshold). So it's a constant subtraction with zero floor. That's what I've been modeling. So the cut-off point is the threshold value. So if the threshold is chosen randomly (e.g., some value), then we derive a single threshold for the day. That's what Attack 7 is.

So my test with C≈152 fails at index 29 because 149.65 < 152, so would be zero, but query is 19.10. So perhaps the reference day is not identical to the normal day for this query. The query day is a different date (August 9, 2012 vs. July 26, 2012 for reference Day 4). The normal week provided is just a reference of typical behavior, not necessarily the exact same day as the query day. The attack detection uses the normal week as a baseline for what is typical. The query day may have a different actual consumption before manipulation, but the attacker might have manipulated the actual consumption of that day using Attack 7 with some threshold. So we shouldn't expect exact arithmetic match. The pattern of zeros at night and reduced during day is characteristic of Attack 7, because night consumption often falls below a threshold and gets zeroed, while day consumption is reduced but retains the shape.

Now consider other attacks. Attack 2 (on-off): zeros for random duration. Here zeros are not random; they are at the start and end, which correspond to low consumption periods. That's not random duration; it's systematic zeros during low-demand hours. That suggests the attacker is avoiding reporting consumption when it would normally be low, possibly to bypass detection based on low consumption patterns? Actually Attack 2 random duration would not align with time of day. So not Attack 2.

Attack 4: a randomly selected period within the day with all measurements reduced. If the selected period is the entire day, then all measurements reduced. That could mean multiplying by a factor (Attack 1) or some reduction. But to get zeros, you'd need some measurements reduced to zero, which would require factor zero for those, but then it's not a consistent reduction across the period. Attack 4 as described doesn't specify reduction method, but it says "All measurements in that period are reduced." Typically in literature, Attack 4 is similar to Attack 1 but localized in time, i.e., multiply by a factor between 0 and 1 during a selected interval. That wouldn't produce zeros unless factor=0, but then all in that interval would be zero. Here we have zeros outside the selected period if the period is the whole day? No, if the whole day is reduced by a factor >0, you wouldn't get zeros. So not Attack 4.

Attack IEEE: random intervals real or zero. The zeros appear in contiguous blocks at start and end, and the non-zero values are not actual, so not IEEE.

Attack 5: completely random pattern based on average consumption. The query day still has a diurnal pattern (ramp up, peak, ramp down), so it's not random.

Attack 6: cap at cutoff. If cutoff is around 110, then high values become 110, low values unchanged. The reference day has many values above 110, so they would be capped to 110. That would result in a day with a flat top at 110, but lower values unchanged (e.g., night values 80-120). But query day night values are zero, not low. So not Attack 6.

Attack 3: multiply each by random factor between 0 and 1. That would scale, so zeros wouldn't occur unless original zero. Not possible.

Attack 1: constant factor, no zeros.

Attack 10: reversal, not applicable.

Attack 12: replacement with lower-consuming user's pattern. Could have zeros if lower user had zero at night. But the shape of the query day still resembles a typical consumption pattern with morning ramp and evening peak, but scaled down and zero at night. It could be a lower-consuming user's pattern, but that user would likely have similar shape but lower magnitude, not necessarily zeros at night. But it's possible. However, Attack 12 is defined as "The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user." That implies the pattern is taken from another user, so it's a real consumption trace from another household. That pattern would likely not have consecutive zeros for hours, because even low-consuming users have some baseline consumption at night (lights, fridge, etc.). In this dataset, even the minimum in reference week is above 76. So a lower-consuming user might still have non-zero night consumption. But we can't rule out completely. However, Attack 7 is the classic "subtract a threshold and floor at zero" attack, which directly explains the pattern of zeros at night and reduced but shaped day consumption. So Attack 7 seems the best fit.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 2 (on-off) would have some zero readings and some normal readings, but query has no zeros and no readings near normal levels (all are lower than normal). So not Attack 2.

Attack 9: all samples replaced by average, resulting in flat profile; not flat.

Considering the variations in the reduction factor, the most plausible are Attack 3 (random factor per sample) or Attack 4 (reduction over a period). Attack 4 typically reduces a randomly selected period, meaning some time block has lower consumption than normal, while other times are normal. In the query day, the entire day seems reduced, not just a period. The morning values are lower (300s vs 500s), midday dip is lower (148 vs 372), evening peak is lower (590 vs 792). So all periods are reduced, not just one. Attack 4 might reduce "all measurements in that period", but if the period is the whole day, it would be like Attack 4 covering the entire day. That seems plausible. But the definition: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." The reduction could be a constant amount or a scaling? It says "reduced", not specifying how. In practice, Attack 4 often means a certain time window is multiplied by a factor or subtracted. If the whole day is the period, then it's essentially Attack 1 or 3 depending on reduction method. But Attack 4 is distinct because it applies over a period, not necessarily per sample or constant factor.

Given the query day is a Monday, and the reference week has a Monday (Day 1) with mean 570, and the query day mean 353, a reduction of about 38%. The reduction is not uniform, but the variation in reduction could be due to random scaling per sample (Attack 3) or a period-specific scaling (Attack 4). Since the entire day is lower, it's likely that the attacker applied a continuous manipulation. Attack 3 (multiplying by different random factors between 0 and 1) would produce a pattern that still looks like the original but with varying amplitude reductions, which matches. Attack 4 might be applied to the entire day as one period, but then it would be a constant factor or offset, which we don't see constant factor. So Attack 3 seems more likely: the attacker injected random scaling to mimic consumption but reduce it.

The shape of query day compared to reference Monday: the morning values are 300s instead of 500s, a difference of ~200; the evening peak 590 vs 792, difference ~200; the dip 148 vs 372, difference ~224. Another possibility is Attack 4: reduction over a randomly selected period. If the period was the whole day, and the reduction was by a constant amount, then it's effectively Attack 7. If reduction was by scaling, then it's Attack 1/3. Attack 4 description: "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify how; could be subtraction or multiplication. But Attack 7 is specifically defined as subtracting a cut-off point. Attack 4 is more generic but typically implies a block of time where consumption is reduced, possibly by a factor. Since the query day shows reduction across the whole day, and the reduction is roughly constant in absolute terms (not proportional), Attack 7 is a better fit.

We should also consider Attack 6: cut-off value, where values above a threshold are capped. That would create a flat top, not seen.

Attack 0: all zeros, no.
Attack 1: constant scaling factor, which would give variable absolute differences. The absolute difference would be proportional to the original value. For high values, difference would be large; for low values, difference small. Here the difference for high values (evening peak) is ~202, and for low values (dip) is ~224, which are similar, not proportional. So not Attack 1.
Attack 2: on-off, no zeros.
Attack 3: random scaling factor per sample. This could produce varying absolute differences, but they would still tend to be larger for larger original values if the scaling factor distribution is similar. With random scaling, the expected difference is (1-factor)*original. So high values would have larger differences on average. Here the differences are similar across the range, suggesting an additive shift.
Attack 5: completely different pattern, not.
Attack 9: constant, not.
Attack 10: reverse pattern, not.
Attack 12: pattern from another user, could be similar but typically would not be a shifted version of own pattern.
Attack IEEE: zeroing some intervals, not.

Thus, Attack 7 seems the most plausible: a cut-off point (constant) is subtracted from each sample, reducing the whole profile. The slight variation in differences could be due to using a reference Monday from a different week, natural variations. The min of query day is 148, no negatives, so the cut-off point was less than the minimum of that day's original consumption. Since reference Monday min is 372, if the cut-off was around 200, it would be safe.

Therefore:
Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack: Most Likely: Attack 7
Confidence: High (given the clear downward shift of whole profile without altering trend)
Reasoning: The consumption pattern preserves the overall shape of a typical Monday (morning and evening peaks, midday dip) but the entire profile is shifted downward by approximately 200 units. This matches Attack 7 where a constant cut-off value is subtracted from each reading. Attack 1 was ruled out because the reduction is not proportional to the original values (absolute differences are similar across the range). Attack 3 was ruled out because random scaling would cause more variability in the shape and would generally produce larger absolute differences at high consumption times.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation: The attacker subtracted a constant value (cut-off point) from each electricity consumption reading. Since no reading became negative, all reported values are positive but reduced. The magnitude is uniformly decreased, resulting in lower total and average consumption, while the trend (temporal pattern) remains the same. Possible technical causes: false data injection (manipulating meter readings by subtracting a fixed amount) or a meter bypass that consistently under-reports by a fixed offset.
